In 1984, University College London replaced its transatlantic satellite links with TCP/IP over IPSS.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Many sites unable to link directly to the Internet created simple gateways for the transfer of electronic mail, the most important application of the time. Sites with only intermittent connections used [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") or [FidoNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FidoNet "FidoNet") and relied on the gateways between these networks and the Internet. Some gateway services went beyond simple mail peering, such as allowing access to [File Transfer Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol "File Transfer Protocol") (FTP) sites via UUCP or mail.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Finally, routing technologies were developed for the Internet to remove the remaining centralized routing aspects. The [Exterior Gateway Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exterior_Gateway_Protocol "Exterior Gateway Protocol") (EGP) was replaced by a new protocol, the [Border Gateway Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border_Gateway_Protocol "Border Gateway Protocol") (BGP). This provided a meshed topology for the Internet and reduced the centric architecture which ARPANET had emphasized. In 1994, [Classless Inter-Domain Routing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classless_Inter-Domain_Routing "Classless Inter-Domain Routing") (CIDR) was introduced to support better conservation of address space which allowed use of [route aggregation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernet "Supernet") to decrease the size of [routing tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Routing_table "Routing table").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

## <span id="TCP.2FIP_goes_global_.281980s.29" class="mw-headline">TCP/IP goes global (1980s)</span>

### <span id="CERN.2C_the_European_Internet.2C_the_link_to_the_Pacific_and_beyond" class="mw-headline">CERN, the European Internet, the link to the Pacific and beyond</span>

Between 1984 and 1988 [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN") began installation and operation of [TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TCP/IP "TCP/IP") to interconnect its major internal computer systems, workstations, PCs and an accelerator control system. CERN continued to operate a limited self-developed system (CERNET) internally and several incompatible (typically proprietary) network protocols externally. There was considerable resistance in Europe towards more widespread use of [TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TCP/IP "TCP/IP"), and the CERN TCP/IP intranets remained isolated from the Internet until 1989.

In 1988, Daniel Karrenberg, from [Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica "Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica") (CWI) in [Amsterdam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amsterdam "Amsterdam"), visited Ben Segal, [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN")'s TCP/IP Coordinator, looking for advice about the transition of the European side of the UUCP Usenet network (much of which ran over X.25 links) over to TCP/IP. In 1987, Ben Segal had met with [Len Bosack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Len_Bosack "Len Bosack") from the then still small company [Cisco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cisco_Systems "Cisco Systems") about purchasing some TCP/IP routers for CERN, and was able to give Karrenberg advice and forward him on to Cisco for the appropriate hardware. This expanded the European portion of the Internet across the existing UUCP networks, and in 1989 CERN opened its first external TCP/IP connections.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 This coincided with the creation of Réseaux IP Européens ([RIPE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RIPE "RIPE")), initially a group of IP network administrators who met regularly to carry out coordination work together. Later, in 1992, RIPE was formally registered as a [cooperative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative "Cooperative") in Amsterdam.

At the same time as the rise of internetworking in Europe, ad hoc networking to ARPA and in-between Australian universities formed, based on various technologies such as X.25 and [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP")Net. These were limited in their connection to the global networks, due to the cost of making individual international UUCP dial-up or X.25 connections. In 1989, Australian universities joined the push towards using IP protocols to unify their networking infrastructures. [AARNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AARNet "AARNet") was formed in 1989 by the [Australian Vice-Chancellors' Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Vice-Chancellors%27_Committee "Australian Vice-Chancellors' Committee") and provided a dedicated IP based network for Australia.

The Internet began to penetrate Asia in the late 1980s. Japan, which had built the UUCP-based network [JUNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JUNET "JUNET") in 1984, connected to [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") in 1989\. It hosted the annual meeting of the [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society"), INET'92, in [Kobe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kobe "Kobe"). [Singapore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Singapore "Singapore") developed TECHNET in 1990, and [Thailand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thailand "Thailand") gained a global Internet connection between Chulalongkorn University and UUNET in 1992.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

### <span id="The_early_global_.22digital_divide.22_emerges" class="mw-headline">The early global “digital divide” emerges</span>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg/360px-InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg "Enlarge")

**[Internet users in 2012 as a percentage of a country's population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_Internet_users "List of countries by number of Internet users")** Source: [International Telecommunications Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telecommunications_Union "International Telecommunications Union").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ITU-IndividualsUsingTheInternet-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Main articles: [Global digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_digital_divide "Global digital divide") and [Digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_divide "Digital divide")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg/360px-FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg "Enlarge")

**[Fixed broadband Internet subscriptions in 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_broadband_Internet_subscriptions "List of countries by number of broadband Internet subscriptions")<br>
as a percentage of a country's population** Source: [International Telecommunications Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telecommunications_Union "International Telecommunications Union").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FixedBroadbandITUDynamic2012-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg/360px-MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg "Enlarge")

**[Mobile broadband Internet subscriptions in 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_broadband_Internet_subscriptions "List of countries by number of broadband Internet subscriptions")<br>
as a percentage of a country's population** Source: [International Telecommunications Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telecommunications_Union "International Telecommunications Union").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-MobleBroadbandITUDynamic2012-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

While developed countries with technological infrastructures were joining the Internet, developing countries began to experience a [digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_divide#Global_digital_divide "Digital divide") separating them from the Internet. On an essentially continental basis, they are building organizations for Internet resource administration and sharing operational experience, as more and more transmission facilities go into place.

#### <span id="Africa" class="mw-headline">Africa</span>

At the beginning of the 1990s, African countries relied upon X.25 [IPSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Packet_Switched_Service "International Packet Switched Service") and 2400 baud modem UUCP links for international and internetwork computer communications.

In August 1995, InfoMail Uganda, Ltd., a privately held firm in Kampala now known as InfoCom, and NSN Network Services of Avon, Colorado, sold in 1997 and now known as Clear Channel Satellite, established Africa's first native TCP/IP high-speed satellite Internet services. The data connection was originally carried by a C-Band RSCC Russian satellite which connected InfoMail's Kampala offices directly to NSN's MAE-West point of presence using a private network from NSN's leased ground station in New Jersey. InfoCom's first satellite connection was just 64 kbit/s, serving a Sun host computer and twelve US Robotics dial-up modems.

In 1996, a [USAID](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USAID "USAID") funded project, the [Leland Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leland_Initiative "Leland Initiative"), started work on developing full Internet connectivity for the continent. [Guinea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea "Guinea"), Mozambique, [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar") and [Rwanda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rwanda "Rwanda") gained [satellite earth stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite_earth_station "Satellite earth station") in 1997, followed by [Ivory Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivory_Coast "Ivory Coast") and [Benin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benin "Benin") in 1998.

Africa is building an Internet infrastructure. [AfriNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AfriNIC "AfriNIC"), headquartered in [Mauritius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mauritius "Mauritius"), manages IP address allocation for the continent. As do the other Internet regions, there is an operational forum, the Internet Community of Operational Networking Specialists.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

There are many programs to provide high-performance transmission plant, and the western and southern coasts have undersea optical cable. High-speed cables join North Africa and the Horn of Africa to intercontinental cable systems. Undersea cable development is slower for East Africa; the original joint effort between [New Partnership for Africa's Development (NEPAD)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NEPAD "NEPAD") and the East Africa Submarine System (Eassy) has broken off and may become two efforts.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

#### <span id="Asia_and_Oceania" class="mw-headline">Asia and Oceania</span>

The [Asia Pacific Network Information Centre (APNIC)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APNIC "APNIC"), headquartered in Australia, manages IP address allocation for the continent. APNIC sponsors an operational forum, the Asia-Pacific Regional Internet Conference on Operational Technologies (APRICOT).

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

In 1991, the People's Republic of China saw its first [TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TCP/IP "TCP/IP") college network, [Tsinghua University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsinghua_University "Tsinghua University")'s TUNET. The PRC went on to make its first global Internet connection in 1994, between the Beijing Electro-Spectrometer Collaboration and [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University")'s Linear Accelerator Center. However, China went on to implement its own digital divide by implementing a country-wide [content filter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China "Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

#### <span id="Latin_America" class="mw-headline">Latin America</span>

As with the other regions, [the Latin American and Caribbean Internet Addresses Registry (LACNIC)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LACNIC "LACNIC") manages the IP address space and other resources for its area. LACNIC, headquartered in Uruguay, operates DNS root, reverse DNS, and other key services.

## <span id="Rise_of_the_global_Internet_.28Late_1980s.2Fearly_1990s_onward.29" class="mw-headline">Rise of the global Internet (Late 1980s/early 1990s onward)</span>

Main article: [Digital revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_revolution "Digital revolution")

### <span id="Opening_the_network_to_commerce" class="mw-headline">Opening the network to commerce</span>

Initially, as with its predecessor networks, the system that would evolve into the Internet was primarily for government and government body use.

However, interest in commercial use of the Internet quickly became a hotly debated topic. Although commercial use was forbidden, the exact definition of commercial use could be unclear and subjective. [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP")Net and the X.25 IPSS had no such restrictions, which would eventually see the official barring of UUCPNet use of [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET") and [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") connections. (Some UUCP links still remained connecting to these networks however, as administrators cast a blind eye to their operation.)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg/360px-Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg "Enlarge")

**Number of Internet hosts worldwide: 1969–2012** Source: [Internet Systems Consortium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Systems_Consortium "Internet Systems Consortium").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

As a result, during the late 1980s, the first [Internet service provider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") (ISP) companies were formed. Companies like [PSINet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSINet "PSINet"), [UUNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUNET "UUNET"), [Netcom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netcom_(USA) "Netcom (USA)"), and [Portal Software](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portal_Software "Portal Software") were formed to provide service to the regional research networks and provide alternate network access, UUCP-based email and [Usenet News](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet") to the public. The first commercial dialup ISP in the United States was [The World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_World_(internet_service_provider) "The World (internet service provider)"), which opened in 1989.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

In 1992, the U.S. Congress passed the Scientific and Advanced-Technology Act, [42 U.S.C.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code "Title 42 of the United States Code") [§ 1862(g)](http://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/1862(g)), which allowed NSF to support access by the research and education communities to computer networks which were not used exclusively for research and education purposes, thus permitting NSFNET to interconnect with commercial networks.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ogc-00-33r_p6-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NSFAUPNote1-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 This caused controversy within the research and education community, who were concerned commercial use of the network might lead to an Internet that was less responsive to their needs, and within the community of commercial network providers, who felt that government subsidies were giving an unfair advantage to some organizations.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NSFNETHearing1992-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

By 1990, ARPANET had been overtaken and replaced by newer networking technologies and the project came to a close. New network service providers including [PSINet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSINet "PSINet"), [Alternet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternet "Alternet"), CERFNet, ANS CO+RE, and many others were offering network access to commercial customers. [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") was no longer the de facto backbone and exchange point for Internet. The [Commercial Internet eXchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Internet_eXchange "Commercial Internet eXchange") (CIX), [Metropolitan Area Exchanges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MAE-East "MAE-East") (MAEs), and later [Network Access Points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Access_Point "Network Access Point") (NAPs) were becoming the primary interconnections between many networks. The final restrictions on carrying commercial traffic ended on April 30, 1995 when the National Science Foundation ended its sponsorship of the NSFNET Backbone Service and the service ended.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 NSF provided initial support for the NAPs and interim support to help the regional research and education networks transition to commercial ISPs. NSF also sponsored the [very high speed Backbone Network Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VBNS "VBNS") (vBNS) which continued to provide support for the supercomputing centers and research and education in the United States.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

### <span id="World_Wide_Web_and_introduction_of_browsers" class="mw-headline">World Wide Web and introduction of browsers</span>

Main articles: [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web"), [Web browser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_browser "Web browser") and [History of the web browser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_web_browser "History of the web browser")

The [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web") (sometimes abbreviated "www" or "W3") is an [information space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_space "Information space") where documents and other [web resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_resources "Web resources") are identified by [URIs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/URI "URI"), interlinked by [hypertext](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypertext "Hypertext") links, and can be accessed via the [Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet "Internet") using a [web browser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_browser "Web browser") and (more recently) [web-based applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 It has become known simply as "the Web". As of the 2010s, the World Wide Web is the primary tool billions use to interact on the Internet, and it has changed people's lives immeasurably.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Precursors to the web browser emerged in the form of [hyperlinked](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperlink "Hyperlink") applications during the mid and late 1980s (the bare concept of hyperlinking had by then existed for some decades). Following these, [Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee "Tim Berners-Lee") is credited with inventing the World Wide Web in 1989 and developing in 1990 both the first [web server](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_server "Web server"), and the first web browser, called [WorldWideWeb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WorldWideWeb "WorldWideWeb") (no spaces) and later renamed Nexus.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Many others were soon developed, with [Marc Andreessen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Andreessen "Marc Andreessen")'s 1993 [Mosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosaic_(web_browser) "Mosaic (web browser)") (later [Netscape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netscape "Netscape")),

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-W3.org-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 being particularly easy to use and install, and often credited with sparking the internet boom of the 1990s.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-bloomberg-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Today, the major web browsers are [Firefox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firefox "Firefox"), [Internet Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Explorer "Internet Explorer"), [Google Chrome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Chrome "Google Chrome"), [Opera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opera_(web_browser) "Opera (web browser)") and [Safari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safari_(web_browser) "Safari (web browser)").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

A boost in web users was triggered in September 1993 by [NCSA Mosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosaic_(web_browser) "Mosaic (web browser)"), a graphical browser which eventually ran on several popular office and home computers.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-mosaic_and_th_w3-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 This was the first web browser aiming to bring multimedia content to non-technical users, and therefore included images and text on the same page, unlike previous browser designs;

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-faq-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 its founder, [Marc Andreessen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Andreessen "Marc Andreessen"), also established the company that in 1994, released [Netscape Navigator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netscape_Navigator "Netscape Navigator"), which resulted in one of the early [browser wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Browser_wars "Browser wars"), when it ended up in a competition for dominance (which it lost) with [Microsoft Windows](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft_Windows "Microsoft Windows")' [Internet Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Explorer "Internet Explorer"). Commercial use restrictions were lifted in 1995\. The online service America Online (AOL) offered their users a connection to the Internet via their own internal browser.

### <span id="Use_in_wider_society_1990s_to_early_2000s_.28Web_1.0.29" class="mw-headline">Use in wider society 1990s to early 2000s (Web 1.0)</span>

During the first decade or so of the public internet, the immense changes it would eventually enable in the 2000s were still nascent. In terms of providing context for this period, [mobile cellular devices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellphone "Cellphone") ("smartphones" and other cellular devices) which today provide near-universal access, were used for business and not a routine household item owned by parents and children worldwide. [Social media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_media "Social media") in the modern sense had yet to come into existence, laptops were bulky and most households did not have computers. Data rates were slow and most people lacked means to video or digitize video so websites such as [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube") did not yet exist, media storage was transitioning slowly from [analog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analog_signal "Analog signal") tape to [digital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_data "Digital data") [optical discs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_disc "Optical disc") ([DVD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DVD "DVD") and to an extent still, [floppy disc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floppy_disc "Floppy disc") to [CD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CD "CD")). Enabling technologies used from the early 2000s such as [PHP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PHP "PHP"), modern [Javascript](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Javascript "Javascript") and [Java](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_(programming_language) "Java (programming language)"), technologies such as [AJAX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AJAX "AJAX"), [HTML 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTML_4 "HTML 4") (and its emphasis on [CSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSS "CSS")), and various [software frameworks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_framework "Software framework"), which enabled and simplified speed of web development, largely awaited invention and their eventual widespread adoption.

The Internet was widely used for [mailing lists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mailing_list "Mailing list"), [emails](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email"), [e-commerce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-commerce "E-commerce") and early popular [online shopping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_shopping "Online shopping") ([Amazon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon.com "Amazon.com") and [eBay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EBay "EBay") for example), [online forums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_forum "Online forum") and [bulletin boards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board "Bulletin board"), and personal websites and [blogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blog "Blog"), and use was growing rapidly, but by more modern standards the systems used were static and lacked widespread social engagement. It awaited a number of events in the early 2000s to change from a communications technology to gradually develop into a key part of global society's infrastructure.

Typical design elements of these "Web 1.0" era websites included:

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Static pages instead of [dynamic HTML](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_HTML "Dynamic HTML");

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 content served from [filesystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filesystem "Filesystem") instead of [relational databases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relational_database "Relational database"); pages built using [Server Side Includes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Server_Side_Includes "Server Side Includes") or [CGI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface "Common Gateway Interface") instead of a [web application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application") written in a [dynamic programming language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language "Dynamic programming language"); [HTML 3.2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTML_3.2 "HTML 3.2")-era structures such as [frames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Framing_(World_Wide_Web) "Framing (World Wide Web)") and tables to create page layouts; online [guestbooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guestbook "Guestbook"); overuse of [GIF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GIF "GIF") buttons and similar small graphics promoting particular items;

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and HTML forms sent via [email](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email"). (Support for [server side scripting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Server_side_scripting "Server side scripting") was rare on [shared servers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_server "Shared server") so the usual feedback mechanism was via email, using [mailto forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mailto "Mailto") and their [email program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email_client "Email client").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

During the period 1997 to 2001, the first [speculative investment](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Speculative_investment&action=edit&redlink=1 "Speculative investment (page does not exist)") [bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Investment_bubble "Investment bubble") related to the Internet took place, in which ["dot-com" companies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_company "Dot-com company") (referring to the "[.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.com ".com")" [top level domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top_level_domain "Top level domain") used by businesses) were propelled to exceedingly high valuations as investors rapidly stoked [stock values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock_value "Stock value"), followed by a [market crash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Market_crash "Market crash"); the first [dot-com bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_bubble "Dot-com bubble"). However this only temporarily slowed enthusiasm and growth, which quickly recovered and continued to grow.

The changes that would propel the Internet into its place as a social system took place during a relatively short period of no more than five years, starting from around 2004\. They included:

- The call to "[Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0 "Web 2.0")" in 2004 (first suggested in 1999),
- Accelerating adoption and commoditization among households of, and familiarity with, the necessary hardware (such as computers).
- Accelerating storage technology and data access speeds – [hard drives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hard_drive "Hard drive") emerged, took over from far smaller, slower [floppy discs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floppy_disc "Floppy disc"), and grew from [megabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megabyte "Megabyte") to [gigabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gigabyte "Gigabyte") (and by around 2010, [terabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terabyte "Terabyte")), [RAM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random-access_memory "Random-access memory") from hundreds of [kilobytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilobyte "Kilobyte") to gigabytes as typical amounts on a system, and [Ethernet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethernet "Ethernet"), the enabling technology for TCP/IP, moved from common speeds of kilobits to tens of megabits per second, to gigabits per second.
- High speed internet and wider coverage of data connections, at lower prices, allowing larger traffic rates, more reliable simpler traffic, and traffic from more locations,
- The gradually accelerating perception of the ability of computers to create new means and approaches to communication, the emergence of social m
edia and websites such as [Twitter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twitter "Twitter") and [Facebook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Facebook "Facebook") to their later prominence, and global collaborations such as [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia "Wikipedia") (which existed before but gained prominence as a result),

and shortly after (approximately 2007–2008 onward):

- The [mobile revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mobile_revolution&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mobile revolution (page does not exist)"), which put the Internet into the hands of much of human society, of all ages, as they travelled around their daily lives, and allowed them to share, discuss, and continually update, inquire, and respond, at almost all times.
- [Non-volatile RAM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-volatile_RAM "Non-volatile RAM") rapidly grew in size and reliability, and decreased in price, becoming a commodity capable of enabling high levels of computing activity on these small handheld devices as well as [solid-state drives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid-state_drive "Solid-state drive") (SSD).
- An emphasis on power efficient processor and device design, rather than purely high processing power; one of the beneficiaries of this was [ARM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARM_Holdings "ARM Holdings"), a British company which had focused since the 1980s on powerful but low cost simple microprocessors. ARM rapidly gained dominance in the market for mobile and embedded devices.

With the call to Web 2.0, the period up to around 2004–2005 was retrospectively named and described by some as Web 1.0.

# <span id="Web_2.0" class="mw-headline">Web 2.0</span>

Main articles: [Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0 "Web 2.0") and [Responsive web design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Responsive_web_design "Responsive web design")

The term "Web 2.0" describes [websites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Website "Website") that emphasize [user-generated content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User-generated_content "User-generated content") (including user-to-user interaction), [usability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usability "Usability"), and [interoperability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_API "Web API"). It first appeared in a January 1999 article called "Fragmented Future" written by Darcy DiNucci, a consultant on [electronic information design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_architecture "Information architecture"), where she wrote:

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-graham-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-oreilly-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DiNucci-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

_"The Web we know now, which loads into a [browser window](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_browser "Web browser") in essentially static screenfuls, is only an [embryo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embryo "Embryo") of the Web to come. The first glimmerings of Web 2.0 are beginning to appear, and we are just starting to see how that embryo might develop. The Web will be understood not as screenfuls of text and graphics but as a transport mechanism, the ether through which interactivity happens. It will [...] appear on your computer screen, [...] on your TV set [...] your car dashboard [...] your cell phone [...] hand-held game machines [...] maybe even your microwave oven."_ The term resurfaced during 2002 – 2004,

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Knorr.2C_Eric_2003-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-jrobb.mindplex.org-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and gained prominence in late 2004 following presentations by [Tim O'Reilly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_O%27Reilly "Tim O'Reilly") and Dale Dougherty at the first [Web 2.0 Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0_Summit "Web 2.0 Summit"). In their opening remarks, [John Battelle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Battelle "John Battelle") and Tim O'Reilly outlined their definition of the "Web as Platform", where software applications are built upon the Web as opposed to upon the desktop. The unique aspect of this migration, they argued, is that "customers are building your business for you".

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-O.27Reilly.2C_Tim_2004-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 They argued that the activities of users generating content (in the form of ideas, text, videos, or pictures) could be "harnessed" to create value.

Web 2.0 does not refer to an update to any technical specification, but rather to cumulative changes in the way Web pages are made and used. Web 2.0 describes an approach, in which sites focus substantially upon allowing users to interact and collaborate with each other in a [social media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_media "Social media") dialogue as creators of [user-generated content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User-generated_content "User-generated content") in a [virtual community](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virtual_community "Virtual community"), in contrast to Web sites where people are limited to the passive viewing of [content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Content_(media_and_publishing) "Content (media and publishing)"). Examples of Web 2.0 include [social networking sites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_networking_site "Social networking site"), [blogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blog "Blog"), [wikis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wiki "Wiki"), [folksonomies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folksonomy "Folksonomy"), [video sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_sharing "Video sharing") sites, [hosted services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_service "Web service"), [Web applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application"), and [mashups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mashup_(web_application_hybrid) "Mashup (web application hybrid)").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 [Terry Flew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terry_Flew "Terry Flew"), in his 3rd Edition of _New Media_ described what he believed to characterize the differences between Web 1.0 and Web 2.0:

"[The] move from personal websites to blogs and blog site aggregation, from publishing to participation, from web content as the outcome of large up-front investment to an ongoing and interactive process, and from content management systems to links based on tagging ([folksonomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folksonomy "Folksonomy"))".

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 This era saw several household names gain prominence through their community-oriented operation – [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube"), Twitter, Facebook, [Reddit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reddit "Reddit") and Wikipedia being some examples.

# <span id="The_mobile_revolution" class="mw-headline">The mobile revolution</span>

Main articles: [History of mobile phones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_mobile_phones "History of mobile phones") and [Mobile Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Web "Mobile Web")

The process of change generally described as "Web 2.0" was itself greatly accelerated and transformed only a short time later by the increasing growth in mobile devices. This [mobile revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mobile_revolution&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mobile revolution (page does not exist)") meant that computers in the form of smartphones became something many people used, took with them everywhere, communicated with, used for photographs and videos they instantly shared or to shop or seek information "on the move" – and used socially, as opposed to items on a desk at home or just used for work.

Location based services, services using location and other sensor information, and [crowdsourcing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crowdsourcing "Crowdsourcing") (frequently but not always location based), became common, with posts tagged by location, or websites and services becoming location aware. Mobile-targeted websites (such as "m.website.com") became common, designed especially for the new devices used. [Netbooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netbook "Netbook"), [ultrabooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultrabook "Ultrabook"), widespread [4G](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4G "4G") and [Wi-Fi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wi-Fi "Wi-Fi"), and mobile chips capable or running at nearly the power of desktops from not many years before on far lower power usage, became enablers of this stage of Internet development, and the term "[App](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/App_(disambiguation) "App (disambiguation)")" emerged (short for "Application program" or "Program") as did the "[App store](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/App_store "App store")".

# <span id="IP_address_exhaustion_and_public_introduction_of_IPv6" class="mw-headline">IP address exhaustion and public introduction of IPv6</span>

Main articles: [IP address exhaustion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IP_address_exhaustion "IP address exhaustion") and [IPv6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPv6 "IPv6")

## <span id="Networking_in_outer_space" class="mw-headline">Networking in outer space</span>

Main article: [Interplanetary Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interplanetary_Internet "Interplanetary Internet")

The first live Internet link into [low earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_earth_orbit "Low earth orbit") was established on January 22, 2010 when astronaut [T. J. Creamer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_Creamer "Timothy Creamer") posted the first unassisted update to his Twitter account from the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station"), marking the extension of the Internet into space.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 (Astronauts at the ISS had used email and Twitter before, but these messages had been relayed to the ground through a NASA data link before being posted by a human proxy.) This personal Web access, which NASA calls the Crew Support LAN, uses the space station's high-speed [Ku band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ku_band "Ku band") microwave link. To surf the Web, astronauts can use a station laptop computer to control a desktop computer on Earth, and they can talk to their families and friends on Earth using [Voice over IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voice_over_IP "Voice over IP") equipment.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Communication with spacecraft beyond earth orbit has traditionally been over point-to-point links through the [Deep Space Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_Space_Network "Deep Space Network"). Each such data link must be manually scheduled and configured. In the late 1990s NASA and Google began working on a new network protocol, [Delay-tolerant networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delay-tolerant_networking "Delay-tolerant networking") (DTN) which automates this process, allows networking of spaceborne transmission nodes, and takes the fact into account that spacecraft can temporarily lose contact because they move behind the Moon or planets, or because [space weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_weather "Space weather") disrupts the connection. Under such conditions, DTN retransmits data packages instead of dropping them, as the standard TCP/IP internet protocol does. NASA conducted the first field test of what it calls the "deep space internet" in November 2008.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Testing of DTN-based communications between the International Space Station and Earth (now termed Disruption-Tolerant Networking) has been ongoing since March 2009, and is scheduled to continue until March 2014.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

This network technology is supposed to ultimately enable missions that involve multiple spacecraft where reliable inter-vessel communication might take precedence over vessel-to-earth downlinks. According to a February 2011 statement by Google's [Vint Cerf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vint_Cerf "Vint Cerf"), the so-called "Bundle protocols" have been uploaded to NASA's [EPOXI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPOXI "EPOXI") mission spacecraft (which is in orbit around the Sun) and communication with Earth has been tested at a distance of approximately 80 light seconds.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

## <span id="Internet_governance" class="mw-headline">Internet governance</span>

Main article: [Internet governance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_governance "Internet governance")

As a [globally distributed network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_network "Global network") of voluntarily interconnected autonomous networks, the Internet operates without a central governing body. It has no centralized governance for either technology or policies, and each constituent network chooses what technologies and protocols it will deploy from the voluntary technical standards that are developed by the [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF).

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 However, throughout its entire history, the Internet system has had an "Internet Assigned Numbers Authority" (IANA) for the allocation and assignment of various technical identifiers needed for the operation of the Internet.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DDN_NIC-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The [Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Corporation_for_Assigned_Names_and_Numbers "Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers") (ICANN) provides oversight and coordination for two principal [name spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Name_space "Name space") in the Internet, the [Internet Protocol address](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IP_address "IP address") space and the [Domain Name System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_Name_System "Domain Name System").

### <span id="NIC.2C_InterNIC.2C_IANA_and_ICANN" class="mw-headline">NIC, InterNIC, IANA and ICANN</span>

Main articles: [InterNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/InterNIC "InterNIC"), [Internet Assigned Numbers Authority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Assigned_Numbers_Authority "Internet Assigned Numbers Authority") and [ICANN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICANN "ICANN")

The IANA function was originally performed by USC Information Sciences Institute, and it delegated portions of this responsibility with respect to numeric network and autonomous system identifiers to the [Network Information Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Information_Center "Network Information Center") (NIC) at [Stanford Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_Research_Institute "Stanford Research Institute") (SRI International) in [Menlo Park, California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Menlo_Park,_California "Menlo Park, California"). In addition to his role as the RFC Editor, [Jon Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Postel "Jon Postel") worked as the manager of IANA until his death in 1998.

As the early ARPANET grew, hosts were referred to by names, and a HOSTS.TXT file would be distributed from [SRI International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International "SRI International") to each host on the network. As the network grew, this became cumbersome. A technical solution came in the form of the [Domain Name System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_Name_System "Domain Name System"), created by [Paul Mockapetris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Mockapetris "Paul Mockapetris"). The Defense Data Network--Network Information Center (DDN-NIC) at SRI handled all registration services, including the [top-level domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top-level_domain "Top-level domain") (TLDs) of [.mil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.mil ".mil"), [.gov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.gov ".gov"), [.edu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.edu ".edu"), [.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.org ".org"), [.net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.net ".net"), [.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.com ".com") and [.us](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.us ".us"), [root nameserver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_nameserver "Root nameserver") administration and Internet number assignments under a [United States Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") contract.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DDN_NIC-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 In 1991, the Defense Information Systems Agency (DISA) awarded the administration and maintenance of DDN-NIC (managed by SRI up until this point) to Government Systems, Inc., who subcontracted it to the small private-sector [Network Solutions, Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Solutions "Network Solutions")

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

The increasing cultural diversity of the Internet also posed administrative challenges for centralized management of the IP addresses. In October 1992, the Internet Engineering Task Force (IETF) published [RFC 1366](https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc1366),

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 which described the "growth of the Internet and its increasing globalization" and set out the basis for an evolution of the IP registry process, based on a regionally distributed registry model. This document stressed the need for a single Internet number registry to exist in each geographical region of the world (which would be of "continental dimensions"). Registries would be "unbiased and widely recognized by network providers and subscribers" within their region. The RIPE Network Coordination Centre (RIPE NCC) was established as the first RIR in May 1992\. The second RIR, the Asia Pacific Network Information Centre (APNIC), was established in Tokyo in 1993, as a pilot project of the Asia Pacific Networking Group.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Cisco-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Since at this point in history most of the growth on the Internet was coming from non-military sources, it was decided that the [Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") would no longer fund registration services outside of the .mil TLD. In 1993 the U.S. [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation"), after a competitive bidding process in 1992, created the [InterNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/InterNIC "InterNIC") to manage the allocations of addresses and management of the address databases, and awarded the contract to three organizations. Registration Services would be provided by [Network Solutions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Solutions "Network Solutions"); Directory and Database Services would be provided by [AT&T](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation "AT&T Corporation"); and Information Services would be provided by [General Atomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Atomics "General Atomics").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

Over time, after consultation with the IANA, the [IETF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IETF "IETF"), [RIPE NCC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RIPE_NCC "RIPE NCC"), [APNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APNIC "APNIC"), and the [Federal Networking Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Networking_Council "Federal Networking Council") (FNC), the decision was made to separate the management of domain names from the management of IP numbers.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Cisco-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Following the examples of RIPE NCC and APNIC, it was recommended that management of IP address space then administered by the InterNIC should be under the control of those that use it, specifically the ISPs, end-user organizations, corporate entities, universities, and individuals. As a result, the [American Registry for Internet Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Registry_for_Internet_Numbers "American Registry for Internet Numbers") (ARIN) was established as in December 1997, as an independent, not-for-profit corporation by direction of the [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation") and became the third Regional Internet Registry.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

In 1998, both the IANA and remaining DNS-related InterNIC functions were reorganized under the control of [ICANN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICANN "ICANN"), a California [non-profit corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-profit_corporation "Non-profit corporation") contracted by the [United States Department of Commerce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce "United States Department of Commerce") to manage a number of Internet-related tasks. As these tasks involved technical coordination for two principal Internet name spaces (DNS names and IP addresses) created by the IETF, ICANN also signed a memorandum of understanding with the IAB to define the technical work to be carried out by the Internet Assigned Numbers Authority.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The management of Internet address space remained with the regional Internet registries, which collectively were defined as a supporting organization within the ICANN structure.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 ICANN provides central coordination for the DNS system, including policy coordination for the split registry / registrar system, with competition among registry service providers to serve each top-level-domain and multiple competing registrars offering DNS services to end-users.

### <span id="Internet_Engineering_Task_Force" class="mw-headline">Internet Engineering Task Force</span>

The [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF) is the largest and most visible of several loosely related ad-hoc groups that provide technical direction for the Internet, including the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB), the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG), and the [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF).

The IETF is a loosely self-organized group of international volunteers who contribute to the engineering and evolution of Internet technologies. It is the principal body engaged in the development of new Internet standard specifications. Much of the IETF's work is done in Working Groups. It does not "run the Internet", despite what some people might mistakenly say. The IETF does make voluntary standards that are often adopted by Internet users, but it does not control, or even patrol, the Internet.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

The IETF started in January 1986 as a quarterly meeting of U.S. government-funded researchers. Non-government representatives were invited starting with the fourth IETF meeting in October 1986\. The concept of Working Groups was introduced at the fifth IETF meeting in February 1987\. The seventh IETF meeting in July 1987 was the first meeting with more than 100 attendees. In 1992, the [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society"), a professional membership society, was formed and IETF began to operate under it as an independent international standards body. The first IETF meeting outside of the United States was held in Amsterdam, The Netherlands, in July 1993\. Today the IETF meets three times a year and attendnce is often about 1,300 people, but has been as high as 2,000 upon occasion. Typically one in three IETF meetings are held in Europe or Asia. The number of non-US attendees is roughly 50%, even at meetings held in the United States.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

The IETF is unusual in that it exists as a collection of happenings, but is not a corporation and has no board of directors, no members, and no dues. The closest thing there is to being an IETF member is being on the IETF or a Working Group mailing list. IETF volunteers come from all over the world and from many different parts of the Internet community. The IETF works closely with and under the supervision of the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG)

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB).

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF) and the [Internet Research Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Steering_Group "Internet Research Steering Group") (IRSG), peer activities to the IETF and IESG under the general supervision of the IAB, focus on longer term research issues.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

#### <span id="Request_for_Comments" class="mw-headline">Request for Comments</span>

[Request for Comments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments") (RFCs) are the main documentation for the work of the IAB, IESG, IETF, and IRTF. [RFC 1](https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc1), "Host Software", was written by Steve Crocker at [UCLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UCLA "UCLA") in April 1969, well before the IETF was created. Originally they were technical memos documenting aspects of ARPANET development and were edited by [Jon Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Postel "Jon Postel"), the first [RFC Editor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RFC_Editor "RFC Editor").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-RFC4844-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

RFCs cover a wide range of information from proposed standards, draft standards, full standards, best practices, experimental protocols, history, and other informational topics.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 RFCs can be written by individuals or informal groups of individuals, but many are the product of a more formal Working Group. Drafts are submitted to the IESG either by individuals or by the Working Group Chair. An RFC Editor, appointed by the IAB, separate from IANA, and working in conjunction with the IESG, receives drafts from the IESG and edits, formats, and publishes them. Once an RFC is published, it is never revised. If the standard it describes changes or its information becomes obsolete, the revised standard or updated information will be re-published as a new RFC that "obsoletes" the original.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-RFC4844-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

### <span id="The_Internet_Society" class="mw-headline">The Internet Society</span>

The [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society") (ISOC) is an international, nonprofit organization founded during 1992 "to assure the open development, evolution and use of the Internet for the benefit of all people throughout the world". With offices near Washington, DC, USA, and in Geneva, Switzerland, ISOC has a membership base comprising more than 80 organizational and more than 50,000 individual members. Members also form "chapters" based on either common geographical location or special interests. There are currently more than 90 chapters around the world.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-isoc.org-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

ISOC provides financial and organizational support to and promotes the work of the standards settings bodies for which it is the organizational home: the [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF), the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB), the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG), and the [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF). ISOC also promotes understanding and appreciation of the [Internet model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_model "Internet model") of open, transparent processes and consensus-based decision-making.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

### <span id="Globalization_and_Internet_governance_in_the_21st_century" class="mw-headline">Globalization and Internet governance in the 21st century</span>

Since the 1990s, the [Internet's governance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_governance "Internet governance") and organization has been of global importance to governments, commerce, civil society, and individuals. The organizations which held control of certain technical aspects of the Internet were the successors of the old ARPANET oversight and the current decision-makers in the day-to-day technical aspects of the network. While recognized as the administrators of certain aspects of the Internet, their roles and their decision-making authority are limited and subject to increasing international scrutiny and increasing objections. These objections have led to the ICANN removing themselves from relationships with first the [University of Southern California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Southern_California "University of Southern California") in 2000,

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and finally in September 2009, gaining autonomy from the US government by the ending of its longstanding agreements, although some contractual obligations with the U.S. Department of Commerce continued.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

The IETF, with financial and organizational support from the Internet Society, continues to serve as the Internet's ad-hoc standards body and issues [Request for Comments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments").

In November 2005, the [World Summit on the Information Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Summit_on_the_Information_Society "World Summit on the Information Society"), held in [Tunis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunis "Tunis"), called for an [Internet Governance Forum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Governance_Forum "Internet Governance Forum") (IGF) to be convened by [United Nations Secretary General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Secretary_General "United Nations Secretary General"). The IGF opened an ongoing, non-binding conversation among stakeholders representing governments, the private sector, civil society, and the technical and academic communities about the future of Internet governance. The first IGF meeting was held in October/November 2006 with follow up meetings annually thereafter.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Since WSIS, the term "Internet governance" has been broadened beyond narrow technical concerns to include a wider range of Internet-related policy issues.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

## <span id="Politicization_of_the_Internet" class="mw-headline">Politicization of the Internet</span>

Due to its prominence and immediacy as an effective means of mass communication, the Internet has also become more [politicized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politicized "Politicized") as it has grown. This has led in turn, to discourses and activities that would once have taken place in other ways, migrating to being mediated by internet.

Examples include political activities such as [public protest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_protest "Mass protest") and [canvassing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canvassing "Canvassing") of support and [votes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vote "Vote"), but also –

- The spreading of ideas and opinions;
- Recruitment of followers, and "coming together" of members of the public, for ideas, products, and causes;
- Providing and widely distributing and sharing information that might be deemed sensitive or relates to [whistleblowing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whistleblowing "Whistleblowing") (and efforts by specific countries to prevent this by [censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_censorship "Internet censorship"));
- [Criminal activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crime "Crime") and [terrorism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrorism "Terrorism") (and resulting [law enforcement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_enforcement "Law enforcement") use, together with its facilitation by [mass surveillance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_surveillance "Mass surveillance")).

### <span id="Net_neutrality" class="mw-headline">Net neutrality</span>

Main article: [Net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality")

On April 23, 2014, the [Federal Communications Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission "Federal Communications Commission") (FCC) was reported to be considering a new rule that would permit [Internet service providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") to offer content providers a faster track to send content, thus reversing their earlier [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality") position.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140423-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140424a-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140511-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 A possible solution to net neutrality concerns may be [municipal broadband](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipal_broadband "Municipal broadband"), according to [Professor Susan Crawford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Susan_P._Crawford "Susan P. Crawford"), a legal and technology expert at [Harvard Law School](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harvard_Law_School "Harvard Law School").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140428-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 On May 15, 2014, the FCC decided to consider two options regarding Internet services: first, permit fast and slow broadband lanes, thereby compromising net neutrality; and second, reclassify broadband as a [telecommunication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunication "Telecommunication") service, thereby preserving net neutrality.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140515a-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140515b-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 On November 10, 2014, [President Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") recommended the FCC reclassify broadband Internet service as a telecommunications service in order to preserve [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20141110-EW-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20141114-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WRD-20150121-DAS-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 On January 16, 2015, [Republicans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States) "Republican Party (United States)") presented legislation, in the form of a [U.S. Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Congress "U.S. Congress") [H. R.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives "United States House of Representatives") [discussion draft bill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_bills_in_the_114th_United_States_Congress "List of bills in the 114th United States Congress"), that makes concessions to net neutrality but prohibits the FCC from accomplishing the goal or enacting any further regulation affecting [Internet service providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") (ISPs).

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150120-JW-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-HG-20150116-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 On January 31, 2015, [AP News](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AP_News "AP News") reported that the FCC will present the notion of applying ("with some caveats") [Title II (common carrier)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_carrier#Telecommunications "Common carrier") of the [Communications Act of 1934](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934 "Communications Act of 1934") to the internet in a vote expected on February 26, 2015.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150202a-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150202b-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150131-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WP-20150102-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150102-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Adoption of this notion would reclassify internet service from one of information to one of [telecommunications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications_service_provider "Telecommunications service provider")

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150204-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and, according to [Tom Wheeler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Wheeler "Tom Wheeler"), chairman of the FCC, ensure [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WRD-20150204-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150206-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The FCC is expected to enforce net neutrality in its vote, according to the [New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_Times "New York Times").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150224-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150225-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

On February 26, 2015, the FCC ruled in favor of [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality") by applying [Title II (common carrier)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_carrier#Telecommunications "Common carrier") of the [Communications Act of 1934](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934 "Communications Act of 1934") and [Section 706](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications_policy_of_the_United_States#Broadband_deployment_policy_objectives "Telecommunications policy of the United States") of the [Telecommunications act of 1996](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934#Telecommunications_Act_of_1996 "Communications Act of 1934") to the Internet.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FCC-20150226-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150226-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150225-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The FCC Chairman, [Tom Wheeler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Wheeler "Tom Wheeler"), commented, "This is no more a plan to regulate the Internet than the [First Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution "First Amendment to the United States Constitution") is a plan to regulate free speech. They both stand for the same concept."

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-HP-20150226-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

On March 12, 2015, the FCC released the specific details of the net neutrality rules.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150312a-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150312b-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FCC-20150315-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 On April 13, 2015, the FCC published the final rule on its new "[Net Neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality_in_the_United_States "Net neutrality in the United States")" regulations.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-CNET-20150413-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FR-20150413-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a>
</sup>
