## <span id="Use_and_culture" class="mw-headline">Use and culture</span>

Main article: [Sociology of the Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sociology_of_the_Internet "Sociology of the Internet")

### <span id="Demographics" class="mw-headline">Demographics</span>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Internet_users_per_100_inhabitants_ITU.svg/360px-Internet_users_per_100_inhabitants_ITU.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_users_per_100_inhabitants_ITU.svg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_users_per_100_inhabitants_ITU.svg "Enlarge")

**Internet users per 100 inhabitants** Source: [International Telecommunications Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telecommunications_Union "International Telecommunications Union").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

See also: [Global Internet usage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Internet_usage "Global Internet usage")

### <span id="Email_and_Usenet" class="mw-headline">Email and Usenet</span>

Main articles: [e-mail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-mail "E-mail"), [Simple Mail Transfer Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_Mail_Transfer_Protocol "Simple Mail Transfer Protocol") and [Usenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet")

Email has often been called the [killer application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killer_application "Killer application") of the Internet. It predates the Internet, and was a crucial tool in creating it. Email started in 1965 as a way for multiple users of a [time-sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-sharing "Time-sharing") [mainframe computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainframe_computer "Mainframe computer") to communicate. Although the history is undocumented, among the first systems to have such a facility were the [System Development Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/System_Development_Corporation "System Development Corporation") (SDC) [Q32](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Q32 "Q32") and the [Compatible Time-Sharing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatible_Time-Sharing_System "Compatible Time-Sharing System") (CTSS) at MIT.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

The ARPANET computer network made a large contribution to the evolution of electronic mail. An experimental inter-system transferred mail on the ARPANET shortly after its creation.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 In 1971 [Ray Tomlinson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Tomlinson "Ray Tomlinson") created what was to become the standard Internet electronic mail addressing format, using the [@ sign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/@ "@") to separate mailbox names from host names.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

A number of protocols were developed to deliver messages among groups of time-sharing computers over alternative transmission systems, such as [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") and [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM")'s [VNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VNET "VNET") email system. Email could be passed this way between a number of networks, including [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET"), [BITNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BITNET "BITNET") and [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET"), as well as to hosts connected directly to other sites via UUCP. See the [history of SMTP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SMTP#History "SMTP") protocol.

In addition, UUCP allowed the publication of text files that could be read by many others. The News software developed by Steve Daniel and [Tom Truscott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Truscott "Tom Truscott") in 1979 was used to distribute news and bulletin board-like messages. This quickly grew into discussion groups, known as [newsgroups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newsgroup "Newsgroup"), on a wide range of topics. On ARPANET and NSFNET similar discussion groups would form via [mailing lists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_mailing_list "Electronic mailing list"), discussing both technical issues and more culturally focused topics (such as science fiction, discussed on the [sflovers](http://www.sflovers.org/) mailing list).

During the early years of the Internet, email and similar mechanisms were also fundamental to allow people to access resources that were not available due to the absence of online connectivity. UUCP was often used to distribute files using the 'alt.binary' groups. Also, [FTP e-mail gateways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FTPmail "FTPmail") allowed people that lived outside the US and Europe to download files using ftp commands written inside email messages. The file was encoded, broken in pieces and sent by email; the receiver had to reassemble and decode it later, and it was the only way for people living overseas to download items such as the earlier Linux versions using the slow dial-up connections available at the time. After the popularization of the Web and the HTTP protocol such tools were slowly abandoned.

### <span id="From_Gopher_to_the_WWW" class="mw-headline">From Gopher to the WWW</span>

Main articles: [History of the World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_World_Wide_Web "History of the World Wide Web") and [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web")

As the Internet grew through the 1980s and early 1990s, many people realized the increasing need to be able to find and organize files and information. Projects such as [Archie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archie_search_engine "Archie search engine"), [Gopher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gopher_(protocol) "Gopher (protocol)"), [WAIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_area_information_server "Wide area information server"), and the FTP Archive list attempted to create ways to organize distributed data. In the early 1990s, Gopher, invented by [Mark P. McCahill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_P._McCahill "Mark P. McCahill") offered a viable alternative to the [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web"). However, in 1993 the World Wide Web saw many advances to indexing and ease of access through search engines, which often neglected Gopher and Gopherspace. As popularity increased through ease of use, investment incentives also grew until in the middle of 1994 the WWW's popularity gained the upper hand. Then it became clear that Gopher and the other projects were doomed fall short.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

One of the most promising [user interface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User_interface "User interface") [paradigms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradigm "Paradigm") during this period was [hypertext](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypertext "Hypertext"). The technology had been inspired by [Vannevar Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vannevar_Bush "Vannevar Bush")'s "[Memex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Memex "Memex")"

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 and developed through [Ted Nelson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ted_Nelson "Ted Nelson")'s research on [Project Xanadu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_Xanadu "Project Xanadu") and [Douglas Engelbart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Engelbart "Douglas Engelbart")'s research on [NLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NLS_(computer_system) "NLS (computer system)").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Many small self-contained hypertext systems had been created before, such as Apple Computer's [HyperCard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HyperCard "HyperCard") (1987). Gopher became the first commonly used hypertext interface to the Internet. While Gopher menu items were examples of hypertext, they were not commonly perceived in that way.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/First_Web_Server.jpg/220px-First_Web_Server.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:First_Web_Server.jpg) [](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:First_Web_Server.jpg "Enlarge")

This [NeXT Computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NeXT_Computer "NeXT Computer") was used by [Sir Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_Tim_Berners-Lee "Sir Tim Berners-Lee") at [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN") and became the world's first [Web server](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_server "Web server").

In 1989, while working at [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN"), [Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee "Tim Berners-Lee") invented a network-based implementation of the hypertext concept. By releasing his invention to public use, he ensured the technology would become widespread.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 For his work in developing the World Wide Web, Berners-Lee received the [Millennium technology prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millennium_technology_prize "Millennium technology prize") in 2004.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 One early popular web browser, modeled after [HyperCard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HyperCard "HyperCard"), was [ViolaWWW](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ViolaWWW "ViolaWWW").

A turning point for the World Wide Web began with the introduction

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 of the [Mosaic web browser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosaic_(web_browser) "Mosaic (web browser)")

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 in 1993, a graphical browser developed by a team at the [National Center for Supercomputing Applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Center_for_Supercomputing_Applications "National Center for Supercomputing Applications") at the [University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign "University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign") (NCSA-UIUC), led by [Marc Andreessen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Andreessen "Marc Andreessen"). Funding for Mosaic came from the _High-Performance Computing and Communications Initiative_, a funding program initiated by the _[High Performance Computing and Communication Act of 1991](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Performance_Computing_and_Communication_Act_of_1991 "High Performance Computing and Communication Act of 1991")_ also known as the _[Gore Bill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gore_Bill "Gore Bill")_.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Mosaic's graphical interface soon became more popular than Gopher, which at the time was primarily text-based, and the WWW became the preferred interface for accessing the Internet. (Gore's reference to his role in "creating the Internet", however, was ridiculed in [his presidential election campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Gore_presidential_campaign,_2000 "Al Gore presidential campaign, 2000"). See the full article [Al Gore and information technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Gore_and_information_technology "Al Gore and information technology")).

Mosaic was superseded in 1994 by Andreessen's [Netscape Navigator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netscape "Netscape"), which replaced Mosaic as the world's most popular browser. While it held this title for some time, eventually competition from [Internet Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Explorer "Internet Explorer") and a variety of other browsers almost completely displaced it. Another important event held on January 11, 1994, was _[The Superhighway Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Superhighway_Summit "The Superhighway Summit")_ at [UCLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UCLA "UCLA")'s Royce Hall. This was the "first public conference bringing together all of the major industry, government and academic leaders in the field [and] also began the national dialogue about the _[Information Superhighway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_Superhighway "Information Superhighway")_ and its implications."

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

_[24 Hours in Cyberspace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/24_Hours_in_Cyberspace "24 Hours in Cyberspace")_, "the largest one-day online event" (February 8, 1996) up to that date, took place on the then-active website, _cyber24.com._

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 It was headed by photographer [Rick Smolan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rick_Smolan "Rick Smolan").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 A photographic exhibition was unveiled at the [Smithsonian Institution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution "Smithsonian Institution")'s [National Museum of American History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Museum_of_American_History "National Museum of American History") on January 23, 1997, featuring 70 photos from the project.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

### <span id="Search_engines" class="mw-headline">Search engines</span>

Main article: [Search engine (computing)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Search_engine_(computing) "Search engine (computing)")

Even before the World Wide Web, there were search engines that attempted to organize the Internet. The first of these was the [Archie search engine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archie_search_engine "Archie search engine") from McGill University in 1990, followed in 1991 by [WAIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_area_information_server "Wide area information server") and Gopher. All three of those systems predated the invention of the World Wide Web but all continued to index the Web and the rest of the Internet for several years after the Web appeared. There are still Gopher servers as of 2006, although there are a great many more web servers.

As the Web grew, [search engines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_search_engine "Web search engine") and [Web directories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_directory "Web directory") were created to track pages on the Web and allow people to find things. The first full-text Web search engine was [WebCrawler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WebCrawler "WebCrawler") in 1994\. Before WebCrawler, only Web page titles were searched. Another early search engine, [Lycos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lycos "Lycos"), was created in 1993 as a university project, and was the first to achieve commercial success. During the late 1990s, both Web directories and Web search engines were popular--[Yahoo!](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahoo! "Yahoo!") (founded 1994) and [Altavista](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altavista "Altavista") (founded 1995) were the respective industry leaders. By August 2001, the directory model had begun to give way to search engines, tracking the rise of [Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google "Google") (founded 1998), which had developed new approaches to [relevancy ranking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relevance_(information_retrieval) "Relevance (information retrieval)"). Directory features, while still commonly available, became after-thoughts to search engines.

Database size, which had been a significant marketing feature through the early 2000s, was similarly displaced by emphasis on relevancy ranking, the methods by which search engines attempt to sort the best results first. Relevancy ranking first became a major issue circa 1996, when it became apparent that it was impractical to review full lists of results. Consequently, [algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithm "Algorithm") for relevancy ranking have continuously improved. Google's [PageRank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PageRank "PageRank") method for ordering the results has received the most press, but all major search engines continually refine their ranking methodologies with a view toward improving the ordering of results. As of 2006, search engine rankings are more important than ever, so much so that an industry has developed ("[search engi
ne optimizers](<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Search_engine_optimization> "Search engine optimization")", or "SEO") to help web-developers improve their search ranking, and an entire body of [case law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Case_law "Case law") has developed around matters that affect search engine rankings, such as use of [trademarks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trademarks "Trademarks") in [metatags](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metatags "Metatags"). The sale of search rankings by some search engines has also created controversy among librarians and consumer advocates.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

On June 3, 2009, [Microsoft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft "Microsoft") launched its new search engine, [Bing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bing "Bing").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The following month Microsoft and [Yahoo!](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahoo! "Yahoo!") announced a deal in which Bing would power [Yahoo! Search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahoo!_Search "Yahoo! Search").

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

# <span id="File_sharing" class="mw-headline">File sharing</span>

Main articles: [File sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_sharing "File sharing"), [Peer-to-peer file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer-to-peer_file_sharing "Peer-to-peer file sharing") and [Timeline of file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_file_sharing "Timeline of file sharing")

Resource or file sharing has been an important activity on computer networks from well before the Internet was established and was supported in a variety of ways including [bulletin board systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board_systems "Bulletin board systems") (1978), [Usenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet") (1980), [Kermit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kermit_(software) "Kermit (software)") (1981), and many others. The [File Transfer Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol "File Transfer Protocol") (FTP) for use on the Internet was standardized in 1985 and is still in use today.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 A variety of tools were developed to aid the use of FTP by helping users discover files they might want to transfer, including the [Wide Area Information Server](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Area_Information_Server "Wide Area Information Server") (WAIS) in 1991, [Gopher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gopher_(protocol) "Gopher (protocol)") in 1991, [Archie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archie_search_engine "Archie search engine") in 1991, [Veronica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veronica_(search_engine) "Veronica (search engine)") in 1992, [Jughead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jughead_(search_engine) "Jughead (search engine)") in 1993, [Internet Relay Chat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat "Internet Relay Chat") (IRC) in 1988, and eventually the [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web") (WWW) in 1991 with [Web directories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_directories "Web directories") and [Web search engines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_search_engines "Web search engines").

In 1999, [Napster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napster "Napster") became the first [peer-to-peer file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer-to-peer_file_sharing "Peer-to-peer file sharing") system.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 Napster used a central server for indexing and peer discovery, but the storage and transfer of files was decentralized. A variety of peer-to-peer file sharing programs and services with different levels of decentralization and [anonymity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anonymity_application "Anonymity application") followed, including: [Gnutella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gnutella "Gnutella"), [eDonkey2000](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EDonkey2000 "EDonkey2000"), and [Freenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freenet "Freenet") in 2000, [FastTrack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FastTrack "FastTrack"), [Kazaa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kazaa "Kazaa"), [Limewire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limewire "Limewire"), and [BitTorrent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BitTorrent_(software) "BitTorrent (software)") in 2001, and [Poisoned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisoned "Poisoned") in 2003.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

All of these tools are general purpose and can be used to share a wide variety of content, but sharing of music files, software, and later movies and videos are major uses.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 And while some of this sharing is legal, large portions are not. Lawsuits and other legal actions caused Napster in 2001, eDonkey2000 in 2005, Kazza in 2006, and Limewire in 2010 to shutdown or refocus their efforts.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 [The Pirate Bay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay "The Pirate Bay"), founded in Sweden in 2003, continues despite a [trial and appeal in 2009 and 2010](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay_trial "The Pirate Bay trial") that resulted in jail terms and large fines for several of its founders.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 File sharing remains contentious and controversial with charges of theft of [intellectual property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intellectual_property "Intellectual property") on the one hand and charges of [censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Censorship "Censorship") on the other.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

# <span id="Dot-com_bubble" class="mw-headline">Dot-com bubble</span>

Main article: [Dot-com bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_bubble "Dot-com bubble")

Suddenly the low price of reaching millions worldwide, and the possibility of selling to or hearing from those people at the same moment when they were reached, promised to overturn established business dogma in advertising, [mail-order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mail-order "Mail-order") sales, [customer relationship management](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Customer_relationship_management "Customer relationship management"), and many more areas. The web was a new [killer app](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killer_app "Killer app")--it could bring together unrelated buyers and sellers in seamless and low-cost ways. Entrepreneurs around the world developed new business models, and ran to their nearest [venture capitalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venture_capitalist "Venture capitalist"). While some of the new entrepreneurs had experience in business and economics, the majority were simply people with ideas, and did not manage the capital influx prudently. Additionally, many dot-com business plans were predicated on the assumption that by using the Internet, they would bypass the distribution channels of existing businesses and therefore not have to compete with them; when the established businesses with strong existing brands developed their own Internet presence, these hopes were shattered, and the newcomers were left attempting to break into markets dominated by larger, more established businesses. Many did not have the ability to do so.

The dot-com bubble burst in March 2000, with the technology heavy [NASDAQ Composite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASDAQ "NASDAQ") index peaking at 5,048.62 on March 10

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 (5,132.52 intraday), more than double its value just a year before. By 2001, the bubble's deflation was running full speed. A majority of the dot-coms had ceased trading, after having burnt through their [venture capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venture_capital "Venture capital") and IPO capital, often without ever making a [profit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Profit_(accounting) "Profit (accounting)"). But despite this, the Internet continues to grow, driven by commerce, ever greater amounts of online information and knowledge and social networking.

# <span id="Dark_net_and_fragmentation_of_the_Internet" class="mw-headline">Dark net and fragmentation of the Internet</span>

Main article: [darknet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darknet "Darknet")

# <span id="Mobile_phones_and_the_Internet" class="mw-headline">Mobile phones and the Internet</span>

See also: [Mobile Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Web "Mobile Web")

The first mobile phone with Internet connectivity was the [Nokia 9000 Communicator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nokia_9000_Communicator "Nokia 9000 Communicator"), launched in Finland in 1996\. The viability of Internet services access on mobile phones was limited until prices came down from that model, and network providers started to develop systems and services conveniently accessible on phones. [NTT DoCoMo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo "NTT DoCoMo") in Japan launched the first mobile Internet service, [i-mode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I-mode "I-mode"), in 1999 and this is considered the birth of the mobile phone Internet services. In 2001, the mobile phone email system by [Research in Motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research_in_Motion "Research in Motion") for their [BlackBerry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BlackBerry "BlackBerry") product was launched in America. To make efficient use of the small screen and [tiny keypad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_keypad "Telephone keypad") and one-handed operation typical of mobile phones, a specific document and networking model was created for mobile devices, the [Wireless Application Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wireless_Application_Protocol "Wireless Application Protocol") (WAP). Most mobile device Internet services operate using WAP. The growth of mobile phone services was initially a primarily Asian phenomenon with Japan, South Korea and Taiwan all soon finding the majority of their Internet users accessing resources by phone rather than by PC.

<sup>[<em>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation\ needed</span></a>
</em>]</sup>

 Developing countries followed, with India, South Africa, Kenya, Philippines, and Pakistan all reporting that the majority of their domestic users accessed the Internet from a mobile phone rather than a PC. The European and North American use of the Internet was influenced by a large installed base of personal computers, and the growth of mobile phone Internet access was more gradual, but had reached national penetration levels of 20–30% in most Western countries.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DasguptaLall2001-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 The cross-over occurred in 2008, when more Internet access devices were mobile phones than personal computers. In many parts of the developing world, the ratio is as much as 10 mobile phone users to one PC user.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

# <span id="Extension_of_Internet_connectivity_to_equipment_generally_.28Internet_of_Things.29" class="mw-headline">Extension of Internet connectivity to equipment generally (Internet of Things)</span>

Main article: [Internet of Things](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_of_Things "Internet of Things")

## <span id="History_of_web_technologies" class="mw-headline">History of web technologies</span>

### <span id="Client_side_.28browser_and_application.29_technologies" class="mw-headline">Client side (browser and application) technologies</span>

[Web pages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_page "Web page") were initially conceived as [structured documents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structured_document "Structured document") based upon [Hypertext Markup Language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypertext_Markup_Language "Hypertext Markup Language") (HTML) which can allow access to [images](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image "Image"), [video](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video "Video"), and other content. [Hyperlinks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperlink "Hyperlink") in the page permit users to [navigate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_navigation "Web navigation") to other pages. In the earliest browsers, images opened in a separate "helper" application. [Marc Andreessen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Andreessen "Marc Andreessen")'s 1993 [Mosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosaic_(web_browser) "Mosaic (web browser)") and 1994 [Netscape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netscape "Netscape")

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-W3.org-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 introduced mixed text and images for non-technical users. HTML evolved during the 1990s, leading to [HTML 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTML_4 "HTML 4") which introduced large elements of [CSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSS "CSS") styling and, later, extensions to allow browser code to make calls and ask for content from servers in a structured way ([AJAX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AJAX "AJAX")).

### <span id="Server_side_technologies" class="mw-headline">Server side technologies</span>

### <span id="Routing_and_networking_technologies" class="mw-headline">Routing and networking technologies</span>

### <span id="Wireless_internet" class="mw-headline">Wireless internet</span>

## <span id="Historiography" class="mw-headline">Historiography</span>

There are nearly insurmountable problems in supplying a [historiography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historiography "Historiography") of the Internet's development. The process of digitization represents a twofold challenge both for historiography in general and, in particular, for historical communication research.

<sup>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a>
</sup>

 A sense of the difficulty in documenting early developments that led to the internet can be gathered from the quote:

> "The Arpanet period is somewhat well documented because the corporation in charge – [BBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBN_Technologies "BBN Technologies") – left a physical record. Moving into the [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") era, it became an extraordinarily decentralized process. The record exists in people's basements, in closets. ... So much of what happened was done verbally and on the basis of individual trust."

> -- Doug Gale (2007)

> <sup>
>   <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a>
> </sup>

  <span class="reference-text">Even after the appropriations act was amended in 1992 to give NSF more flexibility with regard to commercial traffic, NSF never felt that it could entirely do away with its <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation_Network#Acceptable_Use_Policy_.28AUP.29" title="National Science Foundation Network">Acceptable Use Policy</a> and its restrictions on commercial traffic, see the response to Recommendation 5 in NSF’s response to the Inspector General’s review (a April 19, 1993 memo from Frederick Bernthal, Acting Director, to Linda Sundro, Inspector General, that is included at the end of <a href="http://www.nsf.gov/pubs/stis1993/oig9301/oig9301.txt" class="external text">Review of NSFNET</a>, Office of the Inspector General, National Science Foundation, March 23, 1993)</span>

  <span class="reference-text"><a href="http://arstechnica.com/tech-policy/news/2009/09/icann-cuts-cord-to-us-government-gets-broader-oversight.ars" class="external text">ICANN cuts cord to US government, gets broader oversight: ICANN, which oversees the Internet’s domain name system, is a private nonprofit that reports to the US Department of Commerce.
